10134{_}1471{_}I needed the money, that's the simple fact; And the reason you didn't see me at home last summer was because I was busy spending the money I had saved on your invitations and other expensive things."
10134{_}1472{_}Marty usually waited for J.W., but the idea which now occurred to him demanded utterance.
10134{_}1473{_}"Say, Marcia, I think it's fine of you to be studying dispensary work and first aid."
10134{_}1474{_}"How did you know?"
10134{_}1475{_}Marcia demanded.
10134{_}1476{_}"Never mind; I saw Joe Carbrook in Chicago when we went through on our way to the Buckland-Cartwright debate, and I guessed a good deal more than he told me, which wasn't much."
10134{_}1477{_}"Marty," said Marcia, her face aglow and her brave eyes looking into his, "there's nothing secret about it.
10134{_}1478{_}When Joe gets through medical school we shall go out together to whatever field they choose for him.
10134{_}1479{_}The least I can do is to get ready to help."
10134{_}1480{_}"Is that why you've been going to training school?"
10134{_}1481{_}asked J.W.
10134{_}1482{_}They had so long been used to such complete frankness with each other that the question was "taken as meant."
10134{_}1483{_}"Yes, J.W., it is," said Marcia.
10134{_}1484{_}"Joe has been doing perfectly splendid work in his medical course, and they say he will probably turn out to be a wonderful all-round doctor--everybody is surprised at his thoroughness, except me.
10134{_}1485{_}I know what he means by it.
10134{_}1486{_}But, of course, he has little time for training in other sorts of religious work, and so, ever since last June, I've been dividing my time between a settlement dispensary and the training school.
10134{_}1487{_}Why shouldn't I be as keen on my preparation as he is on his, when we're going out to the same work?"
10134{_}1488{_}"You should, Marcia--you should," J.W.
10134{_}1489{_}agreed, vigorously, "and we're proud of you; aren't we, Marty?
10134{_}1490{_}I remember thinking two years ago what fine missionary pioneers you two would make.
10134{_}1491{_}Only trouble is, we'll never know anything about it, after we've once seen your pictures in _The Epworth Herald_ among the recruits of the year.
10134{_}1492{_}If you were only going where a feller could hope to visit you once every two years or so!"
10134{_}1493{_}Marcia looked out across the lake, but she wasn't seeing the white sails that glided along above the rippling blue of its waters.
10134{_}1494{_}In a moment she pulled herself together, and observed that there had been enough talk about a mere visitor.
10134{_}1495{_}"What of you two, now that your student occupation's gone?"
10134{_}1496{_}"Tell her about yourself, Marty," said J.W.
10134{_}1497{_}"She knows what I'm going to do."
10134{_}1498{_}And for the moment it seemed to him a very drab and unromantic prospect, in spite of his agreement with Mr. Drury that all service ranks alike with God.
10134{_}1499{_}Marty was always slow to talk of himself.
10134{_}1500{_}"It isn't much," he said.
10134{_}1501{_}"The district superintendent is asking me to fill out the year on the Ellis and Valencia Circuit--the present pastor is going to Colorado for his health.
10134{_}1502{_}So I'm to be the young circuit-rider," and he smiled a wry little smile.
10134{_}1503{_}He had no conceit of himself to make the appointment seem poor; rather he wondered how any circuit would consent to put up with a boy's crude preaching and awkward pastoral effort.
10134{_}1504{_}But J.W., Jr., was otherwise minded.
10134{_}1505{_}A country circuit for Marty did not accord with his views at all.
10134{_}1506{_}Marty was too good for a country church, he argued, mainly from his memories of the bare little one-room meetinghouse of his early childhood.
10134{_}1507{_}In his periodical trips to the farm he had seen the old church grow older and more forlorn, as one family after another moved away, and the multiplying cars brought the town and its allurements almost to the front gate of every farm.
10134{_}1508{_}So J.W.
10134{_}1509{_}had tried to say "No," for Marty, who would not say it for himself.
10134{_}1510{_}It was one of the rare times when they did not see eye to eye.
10134{_}1511{_}But it made no difference in their sturdy affection; nothing ever could.
10134{_}1512{_}And Marty would take the appointment.
10134{_}1513{_}Commencement over, for the first time in many years the chums went their separate ways, Marty to his circuit, and J.W.
10134{_}1514{_}home to Delafield.
10134{_}1515{_}Then for a little while each had frequent dark-blue days, without quite realizing what made his world so flavorless.
10134{_}1516{_}But that passed, and the young preacher settled down to his preaching, and the young merchant to his merchandising; and soon all things seemed as if they had been just so through the years.
10134{_}1517{_}To J.W.
10134{_}1518{_}came just one indication of the change that college had made.
10134{_}1519{_}Pastor Drury, though he found it wise to do much of his important work in secret, thought to make use of the college-consciousness which most towns possess in June, and which is felt especially, though not confessed, by the college colony.
10134{_}1520{_}The year's diplomas are still very new in June.
10134{_}1521{_}So a college night was announced for the social rooms, with a college sermon to follow on the next Sunday night.
10134{_}1522{_}The League and the Senior Sunday School Department united to send a personal invitation to every college graduate in town, and to every student home for the vacation.
10134{_}1523{_}They responded, four score of them, to the college-night call.
10134{_}1524{_}As J.W.
10134{_}1525{_}moved about and greeted people he had known for years he began to realize that college has its own freemasonry.
10134{_}1526{_}These other graduates were from all sorts of schools; two had been to Harvard, and one to Princeton; several were State University alumni.
10134{_}1527{_}Cartwright was represented by nine, six of them undergraduates, and the others confessed themselves as being from Chicago, Syracuse, De Pauw, three or four sorts of "Wesleyan," Northwestern, Knox, Wabash, Western Reserve, and many more.
10134{_}1528{_}Not even all Methodist, by any means, J.W.
10134{_}1529{_}perceived; and yet the fellowship among these strangers was very real.
10134{_}1530{_}They spoke each other's tongue; they had common interests and common experiences.
10134{_}1531{_}He told himself that here was a suggestion as to the new friends he might make in Delafield, without forgetting the old ones.
10134{_}1532{_}And the prospect of life in Delafield began to take on new values.
10134{_}1533{_}On the next Sunday night not so many college people were out to hear Mr. Drury's straight-thinking and plain-spoken sermon on "What our town asks of its college-trained youth"; and a few of those who came were inclined to resent what they called a lecture on manners and duty.
10134{_}1534{_}But to J.W.
10134{_}1535{_}the sermon was precisely the challenge to service he had been looking for.
10134{_}1536{_}It made up for his feeling at commencement that he was "out of it."
10134{_}1537{_}It completed all which Mr. Drury had suggested at the Institute camp fire four years ago, all that he himself had tried to say at the decision service on the day after the camp fire; all that the pastor had urged two years ago when J.W., Jr., confessed to him his new hesitations and uneasiness.
10134{_}1538{_}The pastor had not preached any great thing.
10134{_}1539{_}He had simply told the college folk in his audience that no matter where they had gone to school, many people had invested much in them, and that the investment was one which in its very nature could not be realized on by the original investors.
10134{_}1540{_}The only possible beneficiaries were either the successive college generations or the communities in which they found their place.
10134{_}1541{_}If they chose to take as personal and unconditional all the benefits of their education, none could forbid them that anti-social choice; but if they accepted education as a trust, a stewardship, something to be used for the common good, they would be worth more to Delafield than all the new factories the Chamber of Commerce could coax to the town.
10134{_}1542{_}And to those who might be interested in this view of education, Pastor Drury said: "Young people of the colleges, you have been trained to some forms of laboratory work, in chemistry, in biology, in geology--yes, even in English.
10134{_}1543{_}I invite you to think of your own town of Delafield as your living laboratory, in which you will be at once experimenters and part of the experiment stuff.
10134{_}1544{_}Look at this town with all its good and evil, its dying powers and its new forces, its dullnesses and its enthusiasms, its folly and wisdom, its old ways and its new people, its wealth and want.
10134{_}1545{_}Do you think it is already becoming a bit of the kingdom of God?
10134{_}1546{_}Or, if you conclude that it seems to be going in ways that lead very far from the Kingdom, do you think it might possess any Kingdom possibilities?
10134{_}1547{_}If you do, no matter what your occupation in Delafield, Delafield itself may be your true vocation, your call from God!"
10134{_}1548{_}For John Wesley Farwell, Jr., it was to become all of that.
10134{_}1549{_}CHAPTER IV   EXPLORING MAIN STREET  J.W., Jr., found small opportunity to make himself obnoxious by becoming a civic missionary before the time.
10134{_}1550{_}He was busy enough with his adjustment to the business life of "Delafield and Madison county," this being the declared commercial sphere of the John W. Farwell Hardware Company.
10134{_}1551{_}J.W.
10134{_}1552{_}always had known hardware, but hitherto in a purely amateur and detached fashion.
10134{_}1553{_}Now he lived with it, from tacks to tractors, ten or twelve hours a day.
10134{_}1554{_}He found that being the son of his father gained him no safe conduct through the shop or with the customers.
10134{_}1555{_}He had a lot to learn, even if he was John Wesley Farwell, Jr. That he was the heir apparent to all this array of cast iron and wrought and galvanized, of tin and wire and steel and aluminum and nickel, did not save him from aching back and skinned knuckles, nor from the various initiations staged by the three or four other employees.
10134{_}1556{_}But he was getting his bearings, and not from the store and the warehouse only.
10134{_}1557{_}A good hardware store in a country town is a center of democracy for town and country alike.
10134{_}1558{_}In what other place do farmers and artisans, country women and city women meet on so nearly equal terms?
10134{_}1559{_}Not in the postoffice, nor in the bank; and certainly not in the department store.
10134{_}1560{_}But the hardware store's customers, men and women all, are masters of the tools they work with; and whoso loves the tools of his craft is brother to every other craftsman.
10134{_}1561{_}It was in the store, therefore, that J.W.
10134{_}1562{_}began to absorb some of the knowledge and acquire some of the experiences that were to make his work something to his town.
10134{_}1563{_}For one thing, he got a new view of local geography, in terms of tools.
10134{_}1564{_}All the farmers from the bottoms of Mill Creek called for pretty much the same implements; the upland farms had different needs.
10134{_}1565{_}The farmers' wives who lived along the route of the creamery wagon had one sort of troubles with tinware; the women of the fruit farms another.
10134{_}1566{_}J.W.
10134{_}1567{_}knew this by the exchange of experiences he listened to while he sold milk strainers and canning outfits.
10134{_}1568{_}He found out that the people on the edge of town who "made garden" were particular about certain tools and equipment which the wheat farmer would not even look at.
10134{_}1569{_}And the townpeople he learned to classify in the same way.
10134{_}1570{_}He was soon on good terms with those store clerks who were handy men about the house, with women who did all their own work, with blacksmiths and carpenters, with unskilled laborers and garage mechanics.
10134{_}1571{_}In time he could almost tell where a man lived and what he did for a living, just by the hardware he bought and the questions he asked about it.
10134{_}1572{_}Heretofore J.W.
10134{_}1573{_}had thought he knew most of the people in Delafield.
10134{_}1574{_}But the first weeks in the store showed him that he knew only a few.
10134{_}1575{_}Up to this time "most of the people in Delafield" had meant, practically, his school friends, the clerks and salespeople in certain stores--and the members of the First Methodist Church.
10134{_}1576{_}That is to say, in the main, to him Delafield had been the church, and the church had been Delafield.
10134{_}1577{_}But now he realized that his church was only a small part of Delafield.
10134{_}1578{_}The town had other churches.
10134{_}1579{_}It had lodges.
10134{_}1580{_}When the store outfitted Odd Fellows' Hall with new window shades he learned that the Odd Fellows shared the place with strong lodges of the Maccabees and Modern Woodmen.
10134{_}1581{_}And there were other halls.
10134{_}1582{_}J.W.
10134{_}1583{_}Farwell, Sr., was a Mason, but these other lodges seemed to have as many members as the Masons, and one or the other of them was always getting ready for a big public display.
10134{_}1584{_}The same condition was true of the country people.
10134{_}1585{_}He began to hear about the Farm Federation, and the Grange, and the Farmers' Elevator, and the cooperative creamery, for members of all of these groups passed in and out of the store.
10134{_}1586{_}One day J.W.
10134{_}1587{_}remarked to the pastor who had dropped into the store: "Mr. Drury, I never noticed before how this place is alive with societies and clubs and lodges and things.
10134{_}1588{_}Everybody seems to belong to three or four organizations.
10134{_}1589{_}And they talk about 'em!
10134{_}1590{_}But I don't hear much about our church, and nothing at all about the old church out at Deep Creek.
10134{_}1591{_}Yet I used to think that the church was the whole thing!"
10134{_}1592{_}The older man nodded.
10134{_}1593{_}"It's true, J.W.," he said, "all the churches together are only a small part of the community.
10134{_}1594{_}They are the best, and usually the best-organized forces we have, I'm sure of that; but the church and the town have to reckon with these others."
10134{_}1595{_}"What good are they all?
10134{_}1596{_}They must cost a pile of money.
10134{_}1597{_}What for?"
10134{_}1598{_}"That's what you might call a whale of a question, J.W."
10134{_}1599{_}John W. Farwell, Senior, who had been standing by, listening, essayed to answer.
10134{_}1600{_}"And you haven't heard yet of all the organizations.
10134{_}1601{_}Look at me, for example.
10134{_}1602{_}I belong to the Chamber of Commerce and the Rotary Club.
10134{_}1603{_}I'm on the Executive Committee of the Madison County Horticultural Society, and I've just retired from the Board of Directors of the Civic League.
10134{_}1604{_}Then you must think of the political parties, and the County Sunday School Association, and the annual Chautauqua, and I don't know what all."
10134{_}1605{_}"Yes, and I notice, dad, that a good many of these," said J.W., Jr., "are just for the men.
10134{_}1606{_}The women must have nearly as many.
10134{_}1607{_}Why, Delafield ought to be a model town, and the country 'round here ought to be a regular paradise, with all these helpers and uplifters on the job.
10134{_}1608{_}But it isn't.
10134{_}1609{_}Maybe they're not all on the job."
10134{_}1610{_}"That's about it, my boy," his father agreed "I sometimes think we need just one more organization--a society that would never meet, but between the meetings of all the other societies would actually get done the things they talk about and pass resolutions about and then go off and forget until the next meeting."
10134{_}1611{_}"Well, dad, what I want to find out," J.W.
10134{_}1612{_}said, as he started off with Mr. Drury to the post office, "is where the church heads in.
10134{_}1613{_}Mr. Drury is sure it has a big responsibility, and maybe it has.
10134{_}1614{_}But what is it willing to do and able to do, and what will the town let it do?
10134{_}1615{_}It seems to me that is the question."
10134{_}1616{_}J.W.
10134{_}1617{_}heard his father's voice echoing after him up the street, "Sure, that is the question," and Mr. Drury added, "Three questions in one."
10134{_}1618{_}J.W.
10134{_}1619{_}found himself taking notice in a way he had not done before through all his years in Delafield.
10134{_}1620{_}As might be expected, he had come home from college with new ideas and new standards.
10134{_}1621{_}The town looked rather more sordid and commonplace than was his boy's remembrance of it.
10134{_}1622{_}Of late it had taken to growing, and a large part of its development had come during his college years.
10134{_}1623{_}So he must needs learn his own town all over again.
10134{_}1624{_}Cherishing his young college graduate's vague new enthusiasm for a better world, he had little sympathy with much that Delafield opinion acclaimed as progress.
10134{_}1625{_}The Delafield Daily Dispatch carried at its masthead every afternoon one or more of such slogans as these: "Be a Delafield Booster," "Boost for more Industries," "Put Delafield on the Map," "Double Delafield in Half a Decade," "Delafield, the Darling of Destiny," "Watch Delafield Grow, but Don't Stop Boosting to Rubber."
10134{_}1626{_}These were taken by many citizens as a sort of business gospel; any "theorist" who ventured to question the wisdom of bringing more people to town, whether the town's business could give them all a decent living or not, was told to sell his hammer and buy a horn.
10134{_}1627{_}J.W.
10134{_}1628{_}said nothing; he was too young and too recent a comer into the town's business life.
10134{_}1629{_}But he could not work up any zeal for this form of town "loyalty."
10134{_}1630{_}A big cannery had been built down near the river, where truck gardens flourished, and there was a new furniture factory at the edge of the freight yards.
10134{_}1631{_}Hereabouts a lot of supremely ugly flats had gone up, two families to each floor and three stories high; and in J.W.
10134{_}1632{_}'s eyes the rubbish and disorder and generally slattern appearance of the region was no great addition to Delafield's attractions.
10134{_}1633{_}Still more did the tumbledown shacks in the neighborhood of the cannery offend the eyes and, to be frank, the ears and nose as well.
10134{_}1634{_}It was a forlorn-looking lot of hovels, occupied by listless, frowsy adults and noisy children.
10134{_}1635{_}Here existence seemed to be a grim caricature of life; the children, the only symbol of abundance to be seen, continued to be grotesque in their very dirt.
10134{_}1636{_}What clothes they had were second or third-hand garments too large for them, which they seemed to be perpetually in danger of losing altogether.
10134{_}1637{_}To J.W., Delafield had always been a town of homes; but in these dismal quarters there was little to answer to the home idea.
10134{_}1638{_}They were merely places where people contrived to camp for a time, longer or shorter; none but a Gradgrind could call them homes.
10134{_}1639{_}One of the factory foremen was a great admirer of Mr. Drury, who introduced him to J.W.
10134{_}1640{_}one day when the foreman had come to the store for some tools.
10134{_}1641{_}He had talked with J.W., and in time a rather casual friendliness developed between them.
10134{_}1642{_}It was this same Foreman Angus MacPherson, a Scot with a name for shrewdness, who gave the boy his first glimpse of what the factory and the cannery meant to Delafield--especially the factory.
10134{_}1643{_}J.W.
10134{_}1644{_}was down at the factory to see about some new band-saws that had been installed; and, his errand finished, he stopped for a chat with Angus.
10134{_}1645{_}"This factory wasn't here when I went off to college," he said.
10134{_}1646{_}"What ever brought it to Delafield?"
10134{_}1647{_}At that MacPherson was off to a perfect start.
10134{_}1648{_}"Ye see, my boy," he began, "Delafield is so central it is a good town for a good-working plant; freights on lumber and finished stuff are not so high as in some places.
10134{_}1649{_}And then there's labor.
10134{_}1650{_}Lots of husky fellows around here want better than farm wages, and they want a chance at town life as well.
10134{_}1651{_}Men from the big cities, with families, hope to find a quieter, cheaper place to live.
10134{_}1652{_}So we've had no trouble getting help.
10134{_}1653{_}Skill isn't essential for most of the work.
10134{_}1654{_}It's not much of a trick nowadays to get by in most factories--the machines do most of the thinking for you, and that's good in some ways.
10134{_}1655{_}Only the men that 'tend the machines can't work up much pride in the output.
10134{_}1656{_}Things go well enough when business is good.
10134{_}1657{_}But when the factory begins to run short time, and lay men off, like it did last winter, there's trouble."
10134{_}1658{_}J.W.
10134{_}1659{_}wanted to know what sort of trouble.
10134{_}1660{_}"Oh, well," said MacPherson, "strikes hurt worst at the time, but strikes are just like boils, a sign of something wrong inside.
10134{_}1661{_}And short-time and lay-offs--well, ye can't expect the factory to go on making golden oak rockers just to store in the sheds.
10134{_}1662{_}Somebody has to buy 'em.
10134{_}1663{_}But the boys ain't happy over four-day weeks, let alone no jobs at all."
10134{_}1664{_}His sociology professor at Cartwright, J.W.
10134{_}1665{_}recalled, had talked a good deal about the labor question, but maybe this foreman knew something about it too.
10134{_}1666{_}So J.W.
10134{_}1667{_}put it up to him: "What is at the bottom of it all, MacPherson?
10134{_}1668{_}What makes the thing the papers call 'labor unrest'?"
10134{_}1669{_}MacPherson hesitated a moment.
10134{_}1670{_}Then he settled himself more comfortably on a pile of boards and proceeded to deliver his soul, or part of it.
10134{_}1671{_}"I can tell you; but there's them that would ship me out of town if I talked too much, so I'll have to be careful.
10134{_}1672{_}John Wesley, you've got a grand name, and the church John Wesley started has a good name, though it's not my church.
10134{_}1673{_}I'm a Scot, you know.
10134{_}1674{_}But I know your preacher, and he and I are of the same mind about this, I know.
10134{_}1675{_}Well, then, if your Methodist Church could find a method with labor, it would get hold of the same sort of common people as the ones who heard Jesus gladly.
10134{_}1676{_}These working-men are not in the way of being saints, ye ken, but they think that somewhere there is a rotten spot in the world of factories and shops and mills.
10134{_}1677{_}They think they learn from experience, who by the way, is the dominie of a high-priced school, that they get most of the losses and few of the profits of industry.
10134{_}1678{_}They get a living wage when times are good.
10134{_}1679{_}When times are bad they lose the one thing they've got to sell, and that's their day's work; when a loafing day is gone there's nothing to show for it, and no way to make it up.
10134{_}1680{_}Maybe that's as it should be, but the worker can't see it, especially if the boss can still buy gasoline and tires when the plant is idle.
10134{_}1681{_}Oh, yes, laddie, I know the working man is headstrong.
10134{_}1682{_}I'll tell you privately, I think he's a fool, because so often he gets into a blind rage and wants to smash the very tools that earn his bite and sup.
10134{_}1683{_}He may have reason to hate some employer, but why hate the job?
10134{_}1684{_}It's a good job, if he makes good chairs.
10134{_}1685{_}He goes on strike, many's the time, without caring that it hurts him and his worse than it hurts the boss.
10134{_}1686{_}And often the boss thinks he wants nothing bigger than a few more things.
10134{_}1687{_}Maybe he _is_ wild for a phonograph and a Ford and golden oak rockers of his own in the parlor, and photographs enlarged in crayon hanging on the walls--and a steady job.
10134{_}1688{_}But, listen to me, John Wesley, Jr., and you'll be a credit to your namesake: these wild, unreasonable workers, with all their foolishness and sometimes wickedness, are whiles dreaming of a different world, a better world for everybody.
10134{_}1689{_}'Twould be no harm if some bosses dreamed more about that too, me boy.
10134{_}1690{_}Your preacher--he's a fine man too, is Mr. Drury--he understands that, and he wants to use it for something to build on.
10134{_}1691{_}That's why I tell folks he's a Methodist preacher with a real method in his ministry.
10134{_}1692{_}Now I'll quit me fashin' and get back to the job.
10134{_}1693{_}I doubt you'll be busy yourself this afternoon."
10134{_}1694{_}He gripped J.W.
10134{_}1695{_}'s hand, so that the knuckles were unable to forget him all day, but what he had said gripped harder than his handshake.
10134{_}1696{_}If the furniture factory was a mixed blessing, what of the cannery?
10134{_}1697{_}Somewhat to his own surprise, J.W.
10134{_}1698{_}was getting interested in his town, but if at first he was inclined to wonder how he happened to develop all this new concern, he soon ceased to think of it.
10134{_}1699{_}So slight a matter could not stay in the front of his thinking when he really began to know something of the Delafield to which he had never paid much attention.
10134{_}1700{_}It was through Joe Carbrook that he got his next jolt.
10134{_}1701{_}Joe, now spending his vacations in ways that amazed people who had memories of his wild younger manner, was in and out of the Farwell store a good deal.
10134{_}1702{_}Also he spent considerable time with Pastor Drury, though there is no record of what they talked about.
10134{_}1703{_}"J.W., old boy," Joe asked one day, coming away from the pastor's study, "have you ever by any chance observed Main Street?"
10134{_}1704{_}"Why, yes," J.W.
10134{_}1705{_}answered, "seeing that two or three or four times a day I walk six blocks of it back and forth to this store door, I suppose I have."
10134{_}1706{_}"Oh, yes, that way," Joe came back at him, "and you've seen me, a thousand times.
10134{_}1707{_}But did you ever observe me?
10134{_}1708{_}My ears, for instance," and he put his hands over them.
10134{_}1709{_}"Which one is the larger?"
10134{_}1710{_}Without in the least understanding what his friend was driving at, and stupidly wondering if he ever had noticed any difference in Joe's ears, J.W.
10134{_}1711{_}stared with inane bewilderment.
10134{_}1712{_}"Is one really larger than the other?"
10134{_}1713{_}he asked, helplessly.
10134{_}1714{_}Joe took his hands down, and laughed.
10134{_}1715{_}"I knew it," he said.
10134{_}1716{_}"You've never observed my ears, and yet you think you have observed Main Street.
10134{_}1717{_}As it happens, each of my ears takes the same-sized ear-muff.
10134{_}1718{_}But you didn't know it.
10134{_}1719{_}Well, never mind ears; I'm thinking about Main Street.
10134{_}1720{_}What do you know of Main Street?"
10134{_}1721{_}J.W.
10134{_}1722{_}thought he could make up for the ear question.
10134{_}1723{_}So he said, boldly, "Joe Carbrook, I can name every place from here to the livery barn north, and from here to the bridge south, on both sides of the street.
10134{_}1724{_}Want me to prove it?"
10134{_}1725{_}"No, J.W., I don't.
10134{_}1726{_}I reckon you can.
10134{_}1727{_}But I believe you're still as blind as I've been about Main Street, just the same.
10134{_}1728{_}I know Chicago pretty well and I doubt if there's as big a percentage of graft and littleness and dollar-pinching and going to the devil generally on State Street or Wabash Avenue as there is an Main Street, Delafield."
10134{_}1729{_}"You're not trying to say that our business men are crooks, are you, Joe?"
10134{_}1730{_}J.W.
10134{_}1731{_}asked, with a touch of resentment.
10134{_}1732{_}"You know I happen to be connected with a business house on Main Street myself."
10134{_}1733{_}"Sure, I know it, and there's Marshall Field's on State Street, and Lyon & Healy's on Wabash Avenue, and Hart, Schaffner & Marx over by the Chicago River; just the same as here.
10134{_}1734{_}But I--well, of course, there's a story back of it all.
10134{_}1735{_}Mother heard a couple of weeks ago that one of our old Epworth League girls was having a hard time of it--she's working at the Racket store, helping to support her folks.
10134{_}1736{_}They've had sickness, and the girl doesn't get big wages.
10134{_}1737{_}So mother asked me to look her up.
10134{_}1738{_}Mother can't get about very easily, you know, and since I'm studying medicine she seems to think I'm the original Mr. Fix-It.
10134{_}1739{_}I made a few discreet inquiries, discreet, that is, for me, and can you guess who that girl is?
10134{_}1740{_}You can't, I know.
10134{_}1741{_}Well, she's Alma Wetherell, and that's the identical girl who gave me such a dressing down one day at the Cartwright Institute four years ago.
10134{_}1742{_}Remember?
10134{_}1743{_}Say, J.W., that day she told me so much of the deadly truth about myself that I hated her even more for knowing what to say than I did for saying it.
10134{_}1744{_}But she had a big lot to do with waking me up, and I owe her something."
10134{_}1745{_}J.W.
10134{_}1746{_}had not remembered the Institute incident.
10134{_}1747{_}But he recalled that Alma was at Cartwright that summer, and he had seen her at church occasionally since he came home from college.
10134{_}1748{_}She was living in town and working in some store or other he knew, but that was all.
10134{_}1749{_}"What did you find out?"
10134{_}1750{_}he asked Joe.
10134{_}1751{_}"I found out enough so that Alma has a better job, and things are going easier at home.
10134{_}1752{_}But that was just a starter.
10134{_}1753{_}My brave John Wesley, do you remember your college sociology and economics and civics and all the rest?
10134{_}1754{_}Never mind confessing; you don't; I didn't either.
10134{_}1755{_}But I began to review 'em in actual business practice.
10134{_}1756{_}First I told the right merchant what sort of a bookkeeper I had found slaving away for ten dollars a week on the dark, smelly balcony of the Racket--and he's given Alma a job at twenty in a sun-lighted office.
10134{_}1757{_}Then I told Mr. Peters of the Racket what I had done, and why.
10134{_}1758{_}He didn't like it, but it will do him good.
10134{_}1759{_}That made me feel able to settle anything, and I'm looking around for my next joy as journeyman rescuer and expert business adjuster.
10134{_}1760{_}Honest, J.W., I've not seen near all there is to see, but I'm swamped already.
10134{_}1761{_}You've got to come along, you and some others, and see for yourself what's the matter with Main Street."
10134{_}1762{_}Not all at once, but before very long, J.W.
10134{_}1763{_}shared Joe's aroused interest.
10134{_}1764{_}Pastor Drury was with them, of course; and the three called into consultation a few other capable and trustworthy men and women.
10134{_}1765{_}Marcia Dayne had come home for a few weeks' holiday, and at once enlisted.
10134{_}1766{_}Alma Wetherell was able to give some highly significant suggestions.
10134{_}1767{_}There was no noise of trumpets, and no publicity of any sort.
10134{_}1768{_}Mr. Drury insisted that what they needed first and most was not newspaper attention, and not even organization, but exact information.
10134{_}1769{_}So for many days a group of puzzled and increasingly astonished people set about the study of their own town's principal street, as though they had never seen it before.
10134{_}1770{_}And, in truth, they never had.
10134{_}1771{_}It was no different from all other small town business districts.
10134{_}1772{_}The Gem Theater vied with the Star and the Orpheum in lavish display of gaudy posters advertising pictures that were "coming to-morrow," and in two weeks of observation the investigators learned what sort of moving pictures Delafield demanded, or, at least what sort it got.
10134{_}1773{_}They took note of the Amethyst Coterie's Saturday night dances--"Wardrobe, 50 cents, Ladies Free"--and of the boys and girls who patronized the place.
10134{_}1774{_}The various cigar and pocket-billiards combinations were quietly observed, some of the observers learning for the first time that young men are so determined to get together that they are not to be deterred by dirt or bad air or foul and brainless talk.
10134{_}1775{_}The candy stores with soda fountains and some of the drug stores which served refreshments took on a new importance.
10134{_}1776{_}Instead of being no more than handy purveyors of sweets, of soft drinks and household remedies, they were seen to be also social centers, places for "dates" and telephone flirtations and dalliance.
10134{_}1777{_}Much of their doings was the merest silly time-killing, but generally the youthful patrons welcomed all this because it was a change from the empty dullness of homes that had missed the home secret, and from the still duller and wasting monotony of uninteresting toil.
10134{_}1778{_}It was Pastor Drury who suggested the explanation for all these forms of profitless and often dangerous amusement.
10134{_}1779{_}He was chatting with the whole group one night, and merely happened to address himself first to J.W., Jr.
10134{_}1780{_}Your great namesake, J.W., was so much a part of his day that he believed with most other great religious thinkers of his time that play was a device of the devil.
10134{_}1781{_}His belief belonged to eighteenth-century theology and psychology.
10134{_}1782{_}But even more it grew out of the vicious diversions of the rich and the brutalizing amusements of the poor.
10134{_}1783{_}Both were bad, and there was not much middle ground.
10134{_}1784{_}But here on Main Street we see people, most of them young, who feel, without always understanding why, that they simply must be amused.
10134{_}1785{_}They feel it so strongly that they will pay any price for it if circumstances won't let them get it any other way.
10134{_}1786{_}And Main Street is ready to oblige them.
10134{_}1787{_}There could be no amusement business if people were not clamoring to be amused.
10134{_}1788{_}And we know now why we have no right to say that all this clamor is the devil's prompting.
10134{_}1789{_}Isn't it queer that the church is only now beginning to believe in the genuineness and wholesomeness of the play instinct, though it is a proper and natural human hunger?
10134{_}1790{_}Literally everybody wants to play.
10134{_}1791{_}"People pay more for the gratification of this hunger than they do for bread or shoes or education or religion.
10134{_}1792{_}They take greater moral risks for it than they do for money.
10134{_}1793{_}We have seen people who undoubtedly are going to the devil by the amusement route, unless something is done to stop them.
10134{_}1794{_}They go wrong quicker and oftener in their play than in their work.
10134{_}1795{_}Are we going to be content with denouncing the dance hall and the poolroom and the vile pictures and the loose conduct of the soft-drink places and Electric Park?
10134{_}1796{_}Haven't we some sort of duty to see that every young person in Delafield has a chance at first-hand, enjoyable, and decent play?"
10134{_}1797{_}All agreed that the pastor was right, though they were not so clear about what could be done.
10134{_}1798{_}But commercialized amusement was not all they found in their quiet voyages of discovery up and down Main Street.
10134{_}1799{_}The chain stores had come to Delafield--not the "5 and 10" only, but stores which specialized in groceries, tobacco, shoes, dry goods, drugs, and other commodities.
10134{_}1800{_}Alongside of them were the locally owned stores.
10134{_}1801{_}Altogether, Main Street had far too many stores to afford good service or reasonable prices.
10134{_}1802{_}With all this duplication on the one hand, and absentee-control on the other, Main Street was a street of underlings--clerks and salespeople and delivery men.
10134{_}1803{_}That condition produced low wages and inefficient methods, many of the workers being too young to be out of school and too dense to show any intelligence about the work they were supposed to do.
10134{_}1804{_}Cheap help was costly, and the efficient help was scarcely to be found at any price.
10134{_}1805{_}The investigators were frankly dismayed at the extent and complexity of the situation.
10134{_}1806{_}They had thought to find occasional cases calling for adjustment, or even for the law.
10134{_}1807{_}But instead they had found a whole fabric of interwoven questions--amusements, wages, competition, cooperation, ignorance, vulgarity, vice, cheapness, trickery, "business is business."
10134{_}1808{_}True, they had found more honest businesses than shady ones, more faithful clerks than shirkers, more decent people in the pleasure resorts than doubtful people.
10134{_}1809{_}But the total of folly and evil was very great; could the church do anything to decrease it?
10134{_}1810{_}And that question led the little company of inquisitive Christians into yet wider reaches of inquiry.
10134{_}1811{_}J.W.
10134{_}1812{_}and Joe and Marcia at Mr. Drury's suggestion agreed to be a sort of unofficial committee to find out about the churches of Delafield.
10134{_}1813{_}He told them that this was first of all a work for laymen.
10134{_}1814{_}The preachers might come in later.
10134{_}1815{_}Joe invited the others to the new Carbrook home on the Heights into which his people had lately moved.
10134{_}1816{_}The Heights was a new thing to J.W.--a rather exclusive residential quarter which had been laid out park-wise in the last four or five years; with houses in the midst of wide lawns, a Heights club house and tennis courts and an exquisite little Gothic church.
10134{_}1817{_}"When our folks first talked about moving out here I thought it was all right; and I do yet, in some ways," explained Joe.
10134{_}1818{_}"But the Heights is getting a little too good for me; I'm not as keen about being exclusive as I used to be.
10134{_}1819{_}I've thought lately that exclusiveness may be just as bad for people inside the gates, as for the people outside.
10134{_}1820{_}But here we are, as the Atlantic City whale said when the ebb tide stranded it in front of the Board Walk.
10134{_}1821{_}What are we up to, us three?"
10134{_}1822{_}"We're up to finding out about the town churches," said J.W.
10134{_}1823{_}"Maybe they can help the town more than they do, but we don't know how, and so far we haven't found anybody else who knows how."
10134{_}1824{_}And Marcia said: "At least we know some things.
10134{_}1825{_}We have the figures.
10134{_}1826{_}About one Delafield citizen in seven goes to church or Sunday school on Sunday.
10134{_}1827{_}Church membership is one in ten.
10134{_}1828{_}And as many people go to the movies and the Columbia vaudeville and the dance halls and poolrooms on Saturday as go to church on Sunday, to say nothing of the crowds that go on the other five days."
10134{_}1829{_}Joe Carbrook whistled.
10134{_}1830{_}"That's a tough nut to crack, gentle people," he said, "because you've simply got to think of those other five days.
10134{_}1831{_}The chances are that four times as many people in Delafield go to other public places as go to church and Sunday school."
10134{_}1832{_}"What can the churches do?"
10134{_}1833{_}asked J.W.
10134{_}1834{_}"You can't make people go to church."
10134{_}1835{_}"No," assented Marcia, "and if you could, it would be foolish.
10134{_}1836{_}We want to make people like the churches, not hate them.
10134{_}1837{_}One thing I believe our churches can do is to put their public services more into methods and forms that don't have to be taken for granted or just mentally dodged.
10134{_}1838{_}Half the time people don't know what a religious service really stands for."
10134{_}1839{_}"Meaning by that----?"
10134{_}1840{_}Joe queried, as much to hear Marcia talk as for the sake of what she might say.
10134{_}1841{_}"Well, they have seen and heard it since they were children.
10134{_}1842{_}When they were little they didn't understand it, and now it is so familiar that they forget they don't understand it," Marcia responded, not wholly oblivious of Joe's strategy, but too much in earnest to care.
10134{_}1843{_}"I've heard of a successful preacher in the East who seems to be making them understand.
10134{_}1844{_}He says he tries to put into each service four things--light, music, motion; that is, change--and a touch of the dramatic.
10134{_}1845{_}Why not?
10134{_}1846{_}I think it could be done without destroying the solemnity of the worship.
10134{_}1847{_}They did it in the Temple at Jerusalem, and they do it in Saint Peter's at Rome and in Westminster Abbey and Saint John's Cathedral in New York.
10134{_}1848{_}Why shouldn't we do it here in our little churches?"
10134{_}1849{_}"Make a note of it, J.W.," ordered Joe.
10134{_}1850{_}"It's worth suggesting to some of the preachers."
10134{_}1851{_}J.W.
10134{_}1852{_}made his note, rather absently, and offered a conclusion of his own:  "The church must take note of the town's sore spots too.
10134{_}1853{_}I've found out that crowding people in tenements and shacks means disease and immorality.
10134{_}1854{_}Isn't that the church's affair?
10134{_}1855{_}Angus MacPherson has taught me that when the jobs are gone little crimes come, followed by bigger ones; and sickness comes too, with the death rate going up.
10134{_}1856{_}Babies are born to unmarried mothers, and babies, with names or without, die off a lot faster in the river shacks and the east side tenements than they do up this way.
10134{_}1857{_}Maybe the church couldn't help all this even if it knew; but I'm for asking it to know."
10134{_}1858{_}"I'll vote for that," Joe asserted, "if you'll vote for my proposition, which is this: our churches must quit trying just to be prosperous; they must quit competing for business like rival barkers at a street fair; they must begin to find out that their only reason for existence is the service they can give to those who need it most; they've got to believe in each other and work with each other and with all the other town forces that are trying to make a better Delafield."
10134{_}1859{_}"That's right," said J.W.
10134{_}1860{_}"I was talking to Mr. Drury this morning, and I asked him what he would think of our starting a suggestion list.
10134{_}1861{_}He said it ought to be a fine thing.
10134{_}1862{_}But he wants us to do it all ourselves.
10134{_}1863{_}Just the same, we can take our suggestions to him, and then, if he believes in them, he can talk to the other preachers about them, and, of course, about any ideas of his own.
10134{_}1864{_}Because you know, I'm pretty sure he has been thinking about all this a good deal longer than we have."
10134{_}1865{_}It was agreed that the list should be started.
10134{_}1866{_}Marcia was not willing to keep it to themselves; she wanted to have it talked about in League and Sunday school and prayer meeting, and then, when everybody had been given the chance to add to it, and to improve on it--but not to weaken it--that it be put out for general discussion among all the churches.
10134{_}1867{_}"And then," said Joe Carbrook, "we might call it 'The Everyday Doctrines of Delafield,' If we stick to the things every citizen will admit he ought to believe and do, the churches will still have all the chance they have now to preach those things which must be left to the individual conscience."
10134{_}1868{_}That was the beginning of a document with which Delafield was to become very familiar in the months which followed; never before had the town been so generally interested in one set of ideas, and to this day you can always start a conversation there by mentioning the "Everyday Doctrines of Delafield," The Methodist preacher gave them their final form, but he took no credit for the substance of them, though, secretly, he was vastly proud that the young people, and especially J.W., should have so thoroughly followed up his first suggestion of a civic creed.
10134{_}1869{_}THE EVERYDAY DOCTRINES OF DELAFIELD  1.
10134{_}1870{_}Every part of Delafield is as much Delafield as any other part We are citizens of a commonwealth, and Delafield should be in fact as well as name a democratic community.
10134{_}1871{_}2.
10134{_}1872{_}Whenever two Delafield citizens can better do something for the town than one could do it, they should get together.
10134{_}1873{_}And the same holds good for twenty citizens, or a hundred, or a thousand.
10134{_}1874{_}One of the town's mottoes should be, "Delafield Is Not Divided."
10134{_}1875{_}3.
10134{_}1876{_}Everything will help Delafield if it means better people, in better homes, with better chances at giving their children the right bringing-up, but anything which merely means more people, or more money, or more business is likely to cost more than it comes to.
10134{_}1877{_}We will boost for Delafield therefore, but we will first be careful.
10134{_}1878{_}4.
10134{_}1879{_}Every part of Delafield is entitled to clean streets and plenty of air, water, and sunlight.
10134{_}1880{_}It is perhaps possible to be a Christian amid ugliness and filth, but it is not easy, and it is not necessary.
10134{_}1881{_}5.
10134{_}1882{_}Every family in Delafield has the right to a place that can be made into a home, at a cost that will permit of family self-respect, proper privacy, and the ordinary decencies of civilized living.
10134{_}1883{_}Every case of poverty in Delafield should be considered as a reflection on the town, as being preventable and curable by remedies which any town that is careful of its good name can apply.
10134{_}1884{_}6.
10134{_}1885{_}Delafield believes that beauty pays better than ugliness.
10134{_}1886{_}Therefore she is for trees and flowers, green lawns, and clean streets, paint where it properly belongs, and everybody setting a good example by caring for his own premises and so inciting his neighbor to outdo him.
10134{_}1887{_}7.
10134{_}1888{_}The only industries Delafield needs are those which can provide for their operation without forcing workers to be idle so much of the time as to reduce apparent income, and so to cause poverty, sickness, and temptation to wrongdoing.
10134{_}1889{_}The standard of income ought to be for the year, and not by the day; in the interest of homes rather than in the interest of lodging houses and lunch rooms.
10134{_}1890{_}8.
10134{_}1891{_}Delafield can support, or should find ways to support, the workers needed in her stores, shops, and factories, at fair pay, without making use of children, who should continue in school, and without reckoning on the desperation of those made poor by their dependence on a job.
10134{_}1892{_}9.
10134{_}1893{_}Amusements in Delafield can be and ought to be clean, self-respecting, and available for everybody.
10134{_}1894{_}This calls for playgrounds and weekday playtime, as well as plenty of recreational opportunities provided by the churches, without money-making features.
10134{_}1895{_}10.
10134{_}1896{_}The forms of amusement provided for pay can be and should be influenced by public opinion, positively expressed, rather than by public indifference.
10134{_}1897{_}Any picture house would rather be praised for bringing a good picture to town than condemned for showing a bad one.
10134{_}1898{_}Picture people enjoy praise as much as preachers do.
10134{_}1899{_}11.
10134{_}1900{_}Delafield's many organizations should tell the whole town what they are trying to do, so that unnecessary duplication of plan and purpose may first be discovered and then done away with.
10134{_}1901{_}12.
10134{_}1902{_}Whenever a Delafield church, or club, or society, proposes to engage in a work that is to benefit the town, the plan ought to be made known, and in due time the results should be published as widely as was the plan.
10134{_}1903{_}This will help us to learn by our Delafield failures as well as by our Delafield successes.
10134{_}1904{_}13.
10134{_}1905{_}The churches of Delafield are Delafield property, as the schools are, though paid for in a different way.
10134{_}1906{_}Neither schools nor churches exist for their own sakes, but for Delafield, and then some.
10134{_}1907{_}14.
10134{_}1908{_}Every church in Delafield should have a definite parish, and every well-defined section or group should have a church.
10134{_}1909{_}The churched should lead in providing for the unchurched, and the overchurched might spare out of their abundance of workers and equipment some of the resources that are needed.
10134{_}1910{_}15.
10134{_}1911{_}The first concern of all the churches should be to reach the unchurched and to make church friends of the church-haters.
10134{_}1912{_}This goes for all the churches; it is more important to get the sense of God and principles of Jesus into the thought of the whole town than to set Protestant and Roman Catholic in mutually suspicious and hateful opposition; devout Jew and sincere Christian must realize that righteousness in Delafield cannot be attended to by either without the other.
10134{_}1913{_}16.
10134{_}1914{_}The churches of Delafield believe that all matters of social concern--work, wages, housing, health, amusement, and morals--are part of every church's business.
10134{_}1915{_}Therefore they will not cease to urge their members always to deal with these matters as Christian citizens, not merely as Christians.
10134{_}1916{_}17.
10134{_}1917{_}Every child and young person in Delafield ought to be in the day school on weekdays, and in Sunday school on Sunday.
10134{_}1918{_}Delafield discourages needless absence from one as much as from the other.
10134{_}1919{_}18.
10134{_}1920{_}Delafield wants the best possible teachers teaching in all her schools.
10134{_}1921{_}She insists on trained teachers on week days, and needs them on Sundays.
10134{_}1922{_}Therefore she believes that teacher-training is part of every church's duty to Delafield.
10134{_}1923{_}"There's one thing about all this that bothers me," said J.W.
10134{_}1924{_}when they had finished the final draft of the Every Day Doctrines, "not that it's the only one; but some of these Doctrines stand small chance of being put into practice until the church people are willing to spend more money on such work.
10134{_}1925{_}It can't be done on the present income of the churches, or by the usual money-raising methods."
10134{_}1926{_}"That's a fact," Joe Carbrook agreed.
10134{_}1927{_}"I'd already made up my mind that the Carbrooks would have to dig a little deeper, and so must everybody else who cares."
10134{_}1928{_}"Yes, but how to get everybody else to care; that's the trouble," J.W.
10134{_}1929{_}persisted.
10134{_}1930{_}"Dad's one of the stewards, you know, and they find it no easy job to collect even what the church needs now.
10134{_}1931{_}They have a deficit to worry with every year, almost."
10134{_}1932{_}Marcia Dayne was the only other member of the "Let's Know Delafield" group who happened to be present at this last meeting.
10134{_}1933{_}She had been waiting for a chance to speak.
10134{_}1934{_}"I'm surprised at you two," she said.
10134{_}1935{_}"Don't you know the only really workable financial way out?"
10134{_}1936{_}"Well, not exactly," J.W.
10134{_}1937{_}admitted.
10134{_}1938{_}"I suppose if we could only get people to care more, they would give more.
10134{_}1939{_}It's a matter of letting them know the need and all that, I guess.
10134{_}1940{_}For instance--"  Marcia was not ready for his "for instances."
10134{_}1941{_}"John Wesley, Jr.," she interrupted with mock severity, "as a thinker you have shone at times with a good deal more brilliance than that.
10134{_}1942{_}If you had said it just the other way 'round you would have been nearer right.
10134{_}1943{_}People _will_ give if they care, of course, but it is even more certain that they will care if they give.
10134{_}1944{_}The thing we need is to show them how to give."
10134{_}1945{_}Joe Carbrook broke into an incredulous laugh.
10134{_}1946{_}"In other words, my fair Marcia, you want Christians to give before they care what it is they are giving to, or even know about it.
10134{_}1947{_}Don't you think our church will be a long time financing the Every Day Doctrines on that system?"
10134{_}1948{_}Joe and Marcia never hesitated to take opposite sides in a discussion, and always with good-humored frankness.
10134{_}1949{_}So Marcia came back promptly: "I know you think it unreasonable," she said, "but there's a condition you overlook.
10134{_}1950{_}We became Christians long before any of us thought about studying Delafield's needs.
10134{_}1951{_}And if we and all the rest of the Christians of the town had accepted our financial relation to the Kingdom and had acted on it from the start, there would always be money enough and to spare."
10134{_}1952{_}"Oh, yes," Joe said understandingly, "I see now.
10134{_}1953{_}You mean the tithe."
10134{_}1954{_}Marcia knew, no matter how, that Joe had begun to think about tithing, and this seemed the opportune time to stress it a little more.
10134{_}1955{_}It could help the Every Day Doctrines, and both Joe and J.W.
10134{_}1956{_}were keen for that.
10134{_}1957{_}So Marcia admitted that she did mean the tithe.
10134{_}1958{_}"I don't pretend to know how it began, any more than I know how real homes were established after the Fall, or how keeping Sunday began; I do know these began long before there was any fourth or fifth commandment, or any Children of Israel.
10134{_}1959{_}And I've gone over all the whole subject with Mr. Drury--he has a lot of practical pamphlets on the tithe.
10134{_}1960{_}I believe that it is the easiest, surest, fairest and cheerfulest way of doing two Christian things at once--acknowledging God's ownership of all we have, and going into partnership with God in his work for the world, what the books sometimes call Christian Stewardship."
10134{_}1961{_}"I'd like to see those pamphlets," said J.W.
10134{_}1962{_}"It's queer you haven't seen them before this," said Marcia.
10134{_}1963{_}"Mr. Drury has distributed hundreds of them.
10134{_}1964{_}But maybe that was when you were away at Cartwright.
10134{_}1965{_}Anyway, I'll get some for you."
10134{_}1966{_}Joe was holding his thought to the main matter.
10134{_}1967{_}"Marcia," said he, "if you can make good on what you said just now, pamphlets or no pamphlets, I'll agree to become a tither.
10134{_}1968{_}First, to start where you did, how is tithing easier than giving whenever you feel like giving?"
10134{_}1969{_}Now, though Marcia expected no such challenge, she was game.
10134{_}1970{_}"I'm not the one to prove all that, but I believe what I said, and I'll try to make good, as you put it.
10134{_}1971{_}But please don't say 'give' when you talk about tithing, or even about any sort of financial plan for Christians.
10134{_}1972{_}The first word is 'pay,' Giving comes afterward.
10134{_}1973{_}Well, then; tithing is the easiest way, because when you are a tither you always have tithing money.
10134{_}1974{_}You begin by setting the tenth apart for these uses, and it is no more hardship to pay it out than to pay out any other money that you have been given with instructions for its use."
10134{_}1975{_}"Not bad, at all," said Joe.
10134{_}1976{_}"Now tell us why it is the surest way of using a Christian's money."
10134{_}1977{_}By this time Marcia was beginning to enjoy herself.
10134{_}1978{_}"It is the surest because it almost collects itself.
10134{_}1979{_}No begging; no schemes.
10134{_}1980{_}You have tithing money on hand--and you have, almost always--therefore you don't need to be coaxed into thinking you can spare it.
10134{_}1981{_}If the cause is a real claim, that's all you need to find out.
10134{_}1982{_}And when you begin to put money into any cause you're going to get interested in that cause.
10134{_}1983{_}Besides, when all Christians tithe there will be more than enough money for every good work."
10134{_}1984{_}J.W.
10134{_}1985{_}had not thought much of the tithe except as being one of those religious fads, and he knew that every church had a few religious faddists.
10134{_}1986{_}But he had long cherished a vast respect for Marcia's good sense, and what she was saying seemed reasonable enough.
10134{_}1987{_}He wondered if it could be backed up by evidence.
10134{_}1988{_}Joe smilingly took up the next excellence of the tithe which Marcia had named.
10134{_}1989{_}"Let me see; did you say that the tithe is the fairest of all Christian financial schemes?"
10134{_}1990{_}"Not that, exactly," Marcia corrected.
10134{_}1991{_}"I said it was the fairest way of acknowledging God's ownership and of working with him in partnership.
10134{_}1992{_}And it is.
10134{_}1993{_}It puts definiteness in the place of whim.
10134{_}1994{_}It is proportional to our circumstances.
10134{_}1995{_}It is not difficult.
10134{_}1996{_}Mr. Drury says that forty years' search has failed to find a tither who has suffered hardship because of paying the tithe."
10134{_}1997{_}"Well, Joe," J.W.
10134{_}1998{_}put in, "if Marcia can produce the evidence on these three points, you may as well take the fourth for granted.
10134{_}1999{_}If tithing is the easiest, surest and fairest plan of Christian Stewardship, seems to me it's just got to be cheerful.
10134{_}2000{_}I'm going to look into it, and if she's right, as I shouldn't wonder, it's up to you and me to get our finances onto the ten per cent basis."
10134{_}2001{_}Joe was never a reluctant convert to anything.
10134{_}2002{_}When he saw the new way, his instinct was for immediate action.
10134{_}2003{_}"Let's go over to Mr. Drury's," he proposed, "and see if we can't settle this thing to-day.
10134{_}2004{_}I hope Marcia's right," and he looked into her eyes with a glance of something more than friendly, "and if she is I'm ready to begin tithing to-day."
10134{_}2005{_}Pastor Drury, always a busy man, reckoned interviews like this as urgent business always.
10134{_}2006{_}Not once nor twice, but many times in the course of a year, his quiet, indirect work resulted in similar expeditions to his study, and as a rule he knew about when to expect them.
10134{_}2007{_}He produced the pamphlets, added a few suggestions of his own, and let the three young people do most of the talking.
10134{_}2008{_}They stayed a long time, no one caring about that.
10134{_}2009{_}As they were thanking the pastor, before leaving, Joe said with his usual directness, "Marcia _was_ right, and here's where I begin to be a systematic Christian as far as my dealings with money are concerned."
10134{_}2010{_}J.W., not in the least ashamed to follow Joe's lead, said, "Same here.
10134{_}2011{_}Wish I'd known it sooner.
10134{_}2012{_}Now we've got to preach it."
10134{_}2013{_}And Joe said to Mr. Drury, in the last moment at the door, "Mr. Drury, if we could all get a conscience about the tithe, and pay attention to that conscience, half the Everyday Doctrines would not even need to be stated.
10134{_}2014{_}They would be self-evident.
10134{_}2015{_}And the other half could be put into practice with a bang!"
10134{_}2016{_}The Delafield _Dispatch_ got hold of a copy of the "Everyday Doctrines" and printed the whole of it with a not unfavorable editorial comment, under the caption "When Will All This Come True?"
10134{_}2017{_}But Walter Drury, when he saw it, said to himself, "It has already come true in a very real sense, for John Wesley, Jr., and these others believe in it."
10134{_}2018{_}And he knew it marked one more stage of the Experiment, so that he could thank God and take courage.
10134{_}2019{_}CHAPTER V   HERE THE ALIEN; THERE THE LITTLE BROWN CHURCH  It was all very well to work out the "Everyday Doctrines of Delafield."
10134{_}2020{_}To secure their adoption and application by all the churches of Delafield was another matter.
10134{_}2021{_}The unofficial committee scattered, for one thing.
10134{_}2022{_}Joe Carbrook went back to medical school, and Marcia to the settlement and the training school.
10134{_}2023{_}Marty was traveling his circuit.
10134{_}2024{_}J. W. and the pastor and a few others continued their studies of the town.
10134{_}2025{_}Nobody had yet ventured to talk about experts, but it began to be evident that the situation would soon require thoroughgoing and skilled assistance.
10134{_}2026{_}Otherwise, all that had been learned would surely be lost.
10134{_}2027{_}One day in the late fall a stranger dropped in at the Farwell Hardware Store and asked for Mr. J.W.
10134{_}2028{_}Farwell, Jr.
10134{_}2029{_}He had called first on Pastor Drury, who was expecting him; and that diplomat had said to him, "Go see J.W.
10134{_}2030{_}I think he'll help you to get something started."
10134{_}2031{_}J.W., with two of the other clerks, was unloading a shipment of stovepipes.
10134{_}2032{_}The marks of his task were conspicuous all over him, and he scarcely looked the part of the public-spirited young Methodist.
10134{_}2033{_}But the visitor was accustomed to know men when he saw them, under all sorts of disguises.
10134{_}2034{_}J.W., called to the front of the store, met the visitor with a good-natured questioning gaze.
10134{_}2035{_}"Mr. Farwell, I am Manford Conover, of Philadelphia.
10134{_}2036{_}Back there we have heard something of the 'Everyday Doctrines of Delafield,' and I've been sent to find out about them--and their authors."
10134{_}2037{_}"Sent?"
10134{_}2038{_}J.W.
10134{_}2039{_}repeated.
10134{_}2040{_}"Why should anybody send you all the way from Philadelphia to Delafield just for that?"
10134{_}2041{_}He could not know how much pastoral and even episcopal planning was back of that afternoon call.
10134{_}2042{_}"Don't think that we reckon it to be unimportant, Mr. Farwell," said Mr. Conover, pleasantly.
10134{_}2043{_}"You see I'm from a Methodist society with a long name and a business as big as its name--the Board of Home Missions and Church Extension.
10134{_}2044{_}The thing some of you are starting here in Delafield is our sort of thing.
10134{_}2045{_}It may supply our Board with new business in its line, and what we can do for you may make your local work productive of lasting results, in other places as well as here."
10134{_}2046{_}J.W.
10134{_}2047{_}did not quite understand, but he was willing to be instructed, for he had found out that the effort to promote the "Everyday Doctrines" was forever developing new possibilities and at the same time revealing new expanses of Delafield ignorance and need.
10134{_}2048{_}Anybody who appeared to have intelligence and interest was the more welcome.
10134{_}2049{_}They talked a while, and then, "I'll tell you what," proposed J.W.
10134{_}2050{_}"How long do you expect to be in town?"
10134{_}2051{_}Mr. Conover replied that as yet he had made no arrangement for leaving.
10134{_}2052{_}"Then let's get together a few people to-night after prayer meeting.
10134{_}2053{_}Our pastor, of course, and the editor of the _Dispatch_--he's the right sort, if he does boost 'boosting' a good deal; and Miss Leigh, of the High School--she's all right every way; and Mrs. Whitehill, the president of the Woman's Association of our church--that's the women's missionary societies and the Ladies' Aid merged into one--she's a regular progressive; and Harry Field, who's just getting hold of his job in the League; and the Sunday school superintendent.
10134{_}2054{_}That's dad, you know; he's had the job for a couple of years now, and he's as keen about it as Harry is over the League."
10134{_}2055{_}They got together, and out of that first simple discussion came all sorts of new difficulties for Delafield Methodism to face and master.
10134{_}2056{_}*       *       *       *       *  Manford Conover was a preacher with a business man's training and viewpoint.
10134{_}2057{_}He may have mentioned his official title, when he first appeared, but nobody remembered it.
10134{_}2058{_}When people couldn't think of his name he was "the man from the Board," which was all the same to him.
10134{_}2059{_}After that first night's meeting Conover gave several days to walks about Delafield.
10134{_}2060{_}J.W.
10134{_}2061{_}had found the shacks and the tenements, and Joe Carbrook had introduced J.W.
10134{_}2062{_}to Main Street, but it was left to Conover to show him Europe and Africa in Delafield.
10134{_}2063{_}There's a certain town in a Middle Western State, far better known than Delafield, rich, intelligent, highly self-content.
10134{_}2064{_}Its churches and schools and clubs are matters for complacent satisfaction.
10134{_}2065{_}And you would be safe in saying that not one in five of its well-to-do people know that the town has a Negro quarter, an Italian section, a Bohemian settlement, a Scandinavian community, a good-sized Greek colony, and some other centers of cultures and customs alien to what they assume is the town's distinctive character.
10134{_}2066{_}They know, of course, that such people live in the town--couldn't help knowing it.
10134{_}2067{_}Their maids are Scandinavian or Negro.
10134{_}2068{_}They buy vegetables and candy from the Greeks.
10134{_}2069{_}They hear of bootlegging and blind tigers among certain foreign groups.
10134{_}2070{_}The rough work of the town is done by men who speak little or no English.
10134{_}2071{_}But all this makes small impression.
10134{_}2072{_}It is a commonplace of American town life.
10134{_}2073{_}And scarcely ever does it present itself as something to be looked into, or needing to be understood.
10134{_}2074{_}So Conover found it to be with Delafield.
10134{_}2075{_}The "Everyday Doctrines" were well enough, but he knew a good deal of spade work must be done before they could take root and grow.
10134{_}2076{_}He fronted a condition which has its counterpart in most American towns, each of which is two towns, one being certain well-defined and delimited areas where languages and Braces live amid conditions far removed from the American notion of what is endurable, and the other the "better part of town," sometimes smugly called "the residence section," where white Americans have homes.
10134{_}2077{_}Conover and Pastor Drury compared notes.
10134{_}2078{_}They were of one mind as to the conditions which Conover had found, conditions not surprising to the minister, who knew more about Delafield than any of his own people suspected.
10134{_}2079{_}One afternoon they met J.W.
10134{_}2080{_}on the street, and he led them into a candy store for hot chocolate.
10134{_}2081{_}As they sipped the chocolate they talked; J.W., as usual, saying whatever he happened to think of.
10134{_}2082{_}"Say, Mr. Conover," he remarked, "I notice in all your talk about the foreigner in America you haven't once referred to the idea of the melting pot.
10134{_}2083{_}Don't you think that's just what America is?
10134{_}2084{_}All these people coming here and getting Americanized and assimilated and all that?"
10134{_}2085{_}"I'd think America was the melting pot if I could see more signs of the melting," Conover answered.
10134{_}2086{_}"But look at Delafield; how much does the melting pot melt here?"
10134{_}2087{_}Then he looked across the store.
10134{_}2088{_}"Do you know the proprietor, Mr.
10134{_}2089{_}Farwell?"
10134{_}2090{_}he asked.
10134{_}2091{_}"Yes, indeed; Nick and I are good friends," answered J.W.
10134{_}2092{_}"Then I wish you'd introduce me," returned Conover.
10134{_}2093{_}"Oh, Nick," J.W.
10134{_}2094{_}called, "will you come over here a minute?"
10134{_}2095{_}Nick came, wiping his hands on his apron.
10134{_}2096{_}"Nick," said J.W., doing the honors, "you know Mr. Drury, the pastor of our church.
10134{_}2097{_}And this is Mr. Conover from Philadelphia, a very good friend of ours.
10134{_}2098{_}He's been looking around town, and wants to ask you something."
10134{_}2099{_}Nick's brisk and cheerful manner was at its best, for he liked J.W., besides liking the trade he brought.
10134{_}2100{_}"Sure," said he, "I tell him anything if I know it.
10134{_}2101{_}Glad for the chance."
10134{_}2102{_}"Mr. Dulas," said Conover--he had taken note of the name on the window, "you know the East Side pretty well, do you?
10134{_}2103{_}Then, you know that many Italians live just north of Linden Street, and there's a block or so of Polish homes between Linden and the next street south?"
10134{_}2104{_}"Sure I do," said Nick, confidently, "I live on other side of them myself.
10134{_}2105{_}See 'em every day."
10134{_}2106{_}"Very well," Conover went on.
10134{_}2107{_}"What I want to know is this: how do the Italians and the Poles get along together?"
10134{_}2108{_}"They don't have nothing much to do with one another," Nick replied.
10134{_}2109{_}"It's like this, the Poles they talk Polish, and maybe a little English.
10134{_}2110{_}The Italians, they speak Italian, and some can talk English, only not much.
10134{_}2111{_}But Poles they can't talk Italian at all, and Italians can't talk Polish.
10134{_}2112{_}So how could they get together?"
10134{_}2113{_}"That's just the question, Mr. Dulas," Conover agreed.
10134{_}2114{_}"I'm telling these gentlemen that it is harder for the different foreign-born people to know one another and to be friendly with one another than it is for them to know and associate with Americans."
10134{_}2115{_}"Sure, Mister," Nick said, with great positiveness.
10134{_}2116{_}"Sure.
10134{_}2117{_}Before I speak English I know nobody but Greeks, and when I start learning English I got no time to learn Polish, or Italian, or whatever it is.
10134{_}2118{_}English I got to speak, if I run a candy store, but not those other languages."
10134{_}2119{_}And he went off to serve a customer who had just entered.
10134{_}2120{_}"There you have that side," said Conover to the minister and J.W.
10134{_}2121{_}"The need of English as an Americanizing force, and the meed of it as a medium of communication between the different foreign groups.
10134{_}2122{_}Looks as though we've got to bear down hard on English, don't you think?"
10134{_}2123{_}"As Nick says, 'Sure I do,'" Mr. Drury assented.
10134{_}2124{_}"It will come out all right with the children, I hope; they're getting the English.
10134{_}2125{_}But it makes things hard just now."
10134{_}2126{_}"What can the church do?"
10134{_}2127{_}J.W.
10134{_}2128{_}put in.
10134{_}2129{_}"Should it undertake to teach English, as that preacher taught Phil Khamis, you remember, Mr. Drury; or Americanization, or what?"
10134{_}2130{_}"I think it should do something else first," said Conover.
10134{_}2131{_}"Why should we Americans try to make Europeans understand us, unless we first try to understand them?
10134{_}2132{_}Isn't ours the first move?"
10134{_}2133{_}"But this is the country they're going to live in," returned J.W.
10134{_}2134{_}"They can't expect us to adjust ourselves to European ways.
10134{_}2135{_}They've got to do the adjusting, haven't they?"
10134{_}2136{_}"Why?"
10134{_}2137{_}Conover came back.
10134{_}2138{_}"Because we were here first?
10134{_}2139{_}But the Indian was here before us.
10134{_}2140{_}We told him he needn't do any adjusting at all, and see what we've made of him.
10134{_}2141{_}Maybe these Europeans can add enriching elements to our American culture."
10134{_}2142{_}"I guess so, but"--and J.W.
10134{_}2143{_}was evidently at a loss--"but they've got to obey our laws, you know, and fit into our civilization.
10134{_}2144{_}The Indian was different.
10134{_}2145{_}We couldn't make Indians of ourselves, and he wouldn't become civilized."
10134{_}2146{_}"Americanized, you mean?"
10134{_}2147{_}and Conover laughed a little at the irony of it.
10134{_}2148{_}"No, no; not that.
10134{_}2149{_}But he wouldn't meet us half way, even," J.W.
10134{_}2150{_}said.
10134{_}2151{_}"I think," suggested Pastor Drury, "that what Mr. Conover means is that we'd better be a little less stiff to newcomers than the Indian was to us.
10134{_}2152{_}Am I right?"
10134{_}2153{_}"Exactly right," returned Conover.
10134{_}2154{_}"Europe is in a general way the mother-land of us all.
10134{_}2155{_}But many of her children were late in getting here.
10134{_}2156{_}The earlier ones have made their contributions; why may not the later ones also bring gifts for our common treasure?"
10134{_}2157{_}"Well, what in particular do you mean?"
10134{_}2158{_}asked J.W., who was finding himself adrift.
10134{_}2159{_}He had been quite willing in the Institute days to be an admirer of Phil Khamis, and to forget that Phil was of alien birth; but this was something more complicated.
10134{_}2160{_}"Particulars are not so simple," Conover said.
10134{_}2161{_}"But, for instance: some European peoples have a fine musical appreciation.
10134{_}2162{_}Some delight in oratory.
10134{_}2163{_}Some are mystical and dreamy.
10134{_}2164{_}Some are very children in their love of color.
10134{_}2165{_}Some are almost artists in their feeling for beauty in their work.
10134{_}2166{_}Some do not enjoy rough play, and others cannot endure to be quiet.
10134{_}2167{_}Some have inherited a passionate love of country, and great traditions of patriotism."
10134{_}2168{_}"We can't value all these things in just the way they do, but at least we can believe that such interests and instincts are worth something to America.
10134{_}2169{_}Then our Americanization work will be not only more intelligent but far more sympathetic."
10134{_}2170{_}"If I may turn to the immediate business," Mr. Drury said with a smile of apology, "suppose you tell J.W.
10134{_}2171{_}what your Board has to suggest for us here in Delafield, Mr.
10134{_}2172{_}Conover?"
10134{_}2173{_}Conover turned to J.W.
10134{_}2174{_}"I wonder if you know anything about Centenary Church?"
10134{_}2175{_}he asked.
10134{_}2176{_}"That little old brick barn over in the East Bottoms?
10134{_}2177{_}Why, yes, or I used to; if was quite a church when I was a youngster, but I haven't been that way lately.
10134{_}2178{_}I guess it's pretty much run down, with all those foreigners moving in.
10134{_}2179{_}Most of the old members have probably moved away.
10134{_}2180{_}I know there were two Methodist boys with me in high school who lived down there, but they've moved up to the Heights.
10134{_}2181{_}One of them lives next to the Carbrooks."
10134{_}2182{_}"Mr. Drury should take you down that way one of these days," said Conover, "and you'd find that when your friends moved out of the church the foreigners who live nearby did not move in.
10134{_}2183{_}Centenary Church is run down, as you say."
10134{_}2184{_}Mr. Drury added, "And the few members who are left don't know which way to turn.
10134{_}2185{_}They have a supply pastor, who isn't able to do much.
10134{_}2186{_}He gets a pitiful salary, but they can't pay more, and there's no money at all, nor any accommodations, for any special attention to the newcomers."
10134{_}2187{_}"Well," said Conover, "I'm instructed to tell you Delafield Methodists that the Board of Home Missions and Church Extension is ready to help make a new Centenary Church, for the people who now live around it.
10134{_}2188{_}We have a department that pays special attention to immigrant and alien populations.
10134{_}2189{_}Our workers know, in general, what is needed.
10134{_}2190{_}We can put some trained people into Centenary, with a pastor who knows how to direct their work.
10134{_}2191{_}I should not be surprised to see a parish house there, and a modernized church building, and a fine array of everyday work being done there."
10134{_}2192{_}"My, but that sounds great, Mr. Drury, doesn't it?"
10134{_}2193{_}asked J.W., in a glow of enthusiasm.
10134{_}2194{_}Then he checked himself.
10134{_}2195{_}"It sounds well enough," he said, "but all that means a lot of money.
10134{_}2196{_}Where's the money to come from?"
10134{_}2197{_}"From you, of course," Conover replied, "but not all or most from you.
10134{_}2198{_}My Board is a benevolent board--that is to say, it is the whole church at work in such enterprises as this.
10134{_}2199{_}That's one way in which its share of the church's benevolent offerings is used"  "But you don't mean to tell us," said J.W., incredulously, "that you can drop in on a place like Delafield, make up your mind what is needed, and then dump a lot of money into a played-out church, just like that?"
10134{_}2200{_}"Oh, it's not so informal as all that," Conover said, "The thing has to go through the official channels, of course.
10134{_}2201{_}Your district superintendent and Brother Drury and the Bishop and several others have had a hand in it already.
10134{_}2202{_}All concerned have agreed as to the needs and possibilities.
10134{_}2203{_}But Delafield is also a good place to put on a demonstration, an actual, operating scheme.
10134{_}2204{_}I have been making ready for a survey of the whole East Side, just a preliminary study, and before anything positive is done we must make a more thorough inquiry.
10134{_}2205{_}We expect to find out everything that needs to be known."
10134{_}2206{_}"There was only one anxiety I had about it," Pastor Drury said, "and that has been all taken away.
10134{_}2207{_}I was keen to have this be a truly Christian demonstration--not just a settlement or a parish house or night school classes, but a real demonstration of Christian service among people who now know little about it.
10134{_}2208{_}In some places these activities are being set going because church people know they ought to do something, and it is easier to give money and have gymnasiums and moving pictures than to make real proof of partnership with Christ by personal service and sacrifice.
10134{_}2209{_}Take your old friend Martin Luther Shenk, J.W.--do you know that he's working at this very difficulty?
10134{_}2210{_}And I hear he's finding, even in the country, that some people will really give themselves, while others will give only their money and their time."
10134{_}2211{_}J.W.
10134{_}2212{_}thought of Win-My-Chum week, and how he had had to drive himself to speak to Marty, so he knew the pastor was right.
10134{_}2213{_}And he went home with all sorts of questions running through his mind, but with no very satisfying answers to make them.
10134{_}2214{_}Coming back in a wakeful night to Mr. Drury's casual mention of Marty, the thought of his chum set him to wondering how that sturdy young itinerant was making it go on the Ellis and Valencia Circuit, just as the pastor guessed it might.
10134{_}2215{_}To wonder was to decide.
10134{_}2216{_}He would take a long-desired holiday.
10134{_}2217{_}A word or two with his father in the morning gave him the excuse for what he wanted to do.
10134{_}2218{_}Then he got Valencia on the long distance, and the operator told him she would find the "Reverend" Shenk for him in a few minutes.
10134{_}2219{_}He had started out that morning to visit along the State Line Highway, as it was part of her business to know.
10134{_}2220{_}At the third try Marty was found, and he answered J.W.
10134{_}2221{_}'s hail with a shout.
